B001JV5BIA|4.0|If you're a horror fan and have been for many years, you probably at some point heard of writing/directing legend Wes Craven's debut film \\"Last House on the Left\\". This low-budget, extremely disturbing (for its time at the very least) 1972 horror film gave rise in various ways to many of the famous horror films that have come since, and it even recently had its own official remake, which I felt handled the overall mood/tone better but was nowhere near as harsh and real. Of course, it's hard and rare to top or even reach the same level of an original film with its remake, but it's especially hard with 70's horror, which always seems so much more real than today's horror films, in which the actors are too recognizable and attractive and the general product too polished. Anyway, this review is about the MGM Collector's Edition DVD release of the original film, though it should be mentioned that the term \\"original\\" should maybe be taken with a grain of salt, as Craven himself admits to having been greatly inspired by Bergman's \\"The Virgin Spring\\", which I have yet to see, but it did sound strikingly similar when described.<br /><br />The plot of \\"Last House on the Left\\" is fairly simple. The day before her 17th birthday party, a sweet, middle-class, girl-next-door type named Mari says goodbye to her parents in their lakeside home and heads out to a rock concert in the city with a female best-friend from the wrong side of the tracks. They decide to try and score some weed before the concert, and they unfortunately run into Junior, the son of just escaped convict Krug, while doing so. Junior lures them into the clutches of Krug and his cohorts, fellow escaped convict \\"Weasel\\", a sex fiend, and Krug's crazy girlfriend, Sadie. Now, if you don't want spoilers, you may want to skip the rest of this synopsis. However, with this film, it's probably best to know what you're getting into. Basically, the gang takes the pair of young girls out into the woods, where they humiliate, torture, rape and murder them. It's pretty graphic and brutal, probably more in how realistic it is rather than really how much is shown. We are talking top-notch, uninhibited acting here on the part of the villains and their two victims, making for a series of scenes so disturbing in their totally realistic cruelty that this film has become legendary. But, the movie doesn't end with the death of the victims, nor is it about the police catching up with them. In fact, the police are the bumbling comic relief here; to an extreme level. The twist of this film is when the killers take refuge in the home of Mari's parents. Once the parents discover whom their distressed houseguests are, \\"Last House on the Left\\" goes into revenge film mode, and it is pretty brutal. In my opinion, it tops its recent remake in this regard, and though the film is excellent in its realism and ability to \\"get to\\" the viewer, the only pleasure I could imagine anyone might gain from viewing this film is in seeing Krug and Company get their comeuppance in the final act.<br /><br />And, that's what makes \\"Last House on the Left\\" a difficult film to review. It is hard to honestly call it a bad film, though it has flaws, naturally, since we are talking about a low-budget, guerilla style, directorial-debut type film here. It has its excellent aspects. It is overall compelling, disturbing and unforgettable. At the same time, however, it is cruel and miserable. There is no joy to be had here, and really, no fun. There is comic relief spliced in throughout, not to mention an intentionally contradictory and sometimes wacky soundtrack from the film's lead baddie, David Hess (Krug), and both cause some discord among viewers (and even some of the film's actors) on the issue of whether or not they \\"work\\" within the context of the film (it almost depends on what sort of film you want this to be, but I often lean towards feeling the comic relief doesn't fit at all and the music, well, I go back and forth on the music, though it no doubt demonstrates that Mr. Hess is truly multitalented. Still, as a study of or comment on human nature, violence and society, it is a fascinating work. It invokes many emotions in the viewer, and it probably wouldn't hurt for young women to see this one when taking those steps towards a more independent lifestyle. The horror of \\"Last House on the Left\\" is that things like this truly do happen, even if they seem too horrible to imagine. There's nothing impossible about what happens to the girls in this film, and in fact, if we're to trust the news media at all, I guess this sort of thing happens every day. It's horrific, and that is why this film is so horrific. Maybe it has little value beyond presenting some of the evils in our society for our own increased awareness, but I suppose that alone makes it worthwhile, and while I find much of this film difficult to watch for just being too tragic, it`s certainly got some great performances worth revisiting too. So, no, I don't hate this one, though it's hard to say I like it. Not sure what to say. I'll just say that \\"Last House on the Left\\", initially or in certain scenes at least, is a hard watch, but you should probably try to watch it at least once.<br /><br />The collector's edition release from MGM, by the way, has some really nice extras. I would have liked a selection of trailers for the film, but we do get a fantastic audio commentary by the 3 male villain actors, some great retrospective featurettes, deleted scenes and never before seen footage. The featurettes and commentary really do a lot to improve one's appreciation of the film, so be sure to watch those. Also, if you are looking for something... sorta similar, and with the same lead baddie in a similar role, I point my finger to \\"House at the Edge of the Park\\".no doubt demonstrates that Mr. Hess is truly multitalented. Still, as a study of or comment on human nature, violence and society, it is a fascinating work. It invokes many emotions in the viewer, and it probably wouldn't hurt for young women to see this one when taking those steps towards a more independent lifestyle. The horror of \\"Last House on the Left\\" is that things like this truly do happen, even if they seem too horrible to imagine. There's nothing impossible about what happens to the girls in this film, and in fact, if we're to trust the news media at all, I guess this sort of thing happens every day. It's horrific, and that is why this film is so horrific. Maybe it has little value beyond presenting some of the evils in our society for our own increased awareness, but I suppose that alone makes it worthwhile, and while I find much of this film difficult to watch for just being too tragic, it`s certainly got some great performances worth revisiting too. So, no, I don't hate this one, though it's hard to say I like it. Not sure what to say. I'll just say that \\"Last House on the Left\\", initially or in certain scenes at least, is a hard watch, but you should probably try to watch it at least once.   The collector's edition release from MGM, by the way, has some really nice extras. I would have liked a selection of trailers for the film, but we do get a fantastic audio commentary by the 3 male villain actors, some great retrospective featurettes, deleted scenes and never before seen footage. The featurettes and commentary really do a lot to improve one's appreciation of the film, so be sure to watch those. Also, if you are looking for something... sorta similar, and with the same lead baddie in a similar role, I point my finger to \\"House at the Edge of the Park\\".

1594202842|5.0|A very enjoyable and quick read.  The outrageous behavior of the mother (at least in comparison to western norms) had me laughing and horrified at the same time. It led both my wife and I into a lot of interesting discussions around our own child rearing practices.  At the end of the book, Lulu (the 'rebellious' daughter) makes the interesting observation that although she hated being pushed into the violin by her mother she appreciated the experience.  She remarks something along the lines \\"American parents think they are allowing their kids to pursue their dreams, but most kids are just watching TV or on facebook all day.\\"  There is definitely something in the author's \\"virtuous circle\\" logic that young children can't make good decisions and that they need to be pushed over the hump to start enjoying something.    An interesting contrast is Earl Woods (father of Tiger) book, where he emphasizes not pushing the child, but making the child interested, then getting the child to ask the parent for the privilege of being allowed to practice.       A lot of the negative reviews here seem to be reactions to the WSJ piece and the author's child rearing practices.  The book is more nuanced and you see a more balanced portrayal of the pros and the cons of \\"chinese parenting\\".  And irrespective of what you feel about the correctness of her parenting, the author does an excellent job of transporting you into her world and letting you see through her eyes.  An entertaining, thought-provoking book and well worth a read.    The kindle edition as always is excellent.  But, I still find it hard to stomach the Kindle editions being pretty much the same price as the physical book which needs to be printed, stored, then shipped to my house.

Of future interest to us will be the development of the defence of `adequate provisions' for companies accused of committing the corporate.<br /><br />This Guide is extremely timely although, as Lord Woolf suggests, it's clear that there will be demands for further editions as we see how the new law settles down, and whether it has worked or not. The bulk of the book covers an introduction and background to the general bribery offences and then looks specifically at the following important areas: bribery of foreign public officials; the failure of commercial organisations to prevent bribery; defences; facilitation payments, corporate hospitality, promotional expenditure, and commission payments; consent to prosecute; outcomes, penalties and remedies; investigation and prosecution; and international and domestic instruments.<br /><br />Monty Raphael has set out a quick reference tool here which is logically organized and follows the structure of the Act which is set out in the first Appendix. 140574989X|5.0|[[VIDEOID:mo11N8VFO4BPDB9]]FOR THE SECOND DECADE OF 21st CENTURY<br /><br />An appreciation by Phillip Taylor MBE and Elizabeth Taylor of Richmond Green Chambers<br /><br />It is a big issue for the new Coalition Government: legal services- what are they going cost?  What should they cost in 2011?  How are costs determined?  Where do you go for authoritative, well informed, reliable advice?<br /><br />Well, whether you're a solicitor, barrister, judge, or cost draftsman, your first port of call will almost certainly be \\"Cook on Costs 2011\\", and it is easily the least formidable for most professionals associated with legal matters today.<br /><br />Michael Cook has long been known for his expertise in this complicated area which inevitably lends itself all too often to dissension and dispute.  But here you have Cook's up to date, authoritative and easily accessible work which focuses on the practical aspects of costs, with clear, straightforward explanation and examination of every aspect of the remuneration of solicitors and barristers for work done.<br /><br />Thoroughly and comprehensively, Cook covers the entitlement and quantification of contentious and non-contentious legal business brilliantly, and it includes costs between the parties and family costs.  Private and public funding of litigation is also covered and the text is helpfully illustrated with decided cases, many of which you won't find in the law reports.  There are verbatim extracts from all landmark costs judgments and all the relevant statutes, rules and regulations governing civil costs are also included.<br /><br />Law Society President Michael Napier has put it succinctly saying that the funding of litigation - whether the case can be funded and who pays what to whom at the end - has become a new and essential skill for litigators everywhere!<br /><br />Fortunately, \\"Cook on Costs\\" which has been with us since 1991, is now annual helping the profession `steer its way through the costs maze' very effectively.  Napier has added that due to the `dizzying pace of change'- recalling that the law lords have gone, and solicitors are no longer `of the Supreme Court' but `of the Senior Courts' to site only two examples- a reference work like this needs to be published yearly. So, that is what \\"Cook on Costs\\" actually is today: the 2011 annual edition of this familiar title, which first came on the scene 20 years ago.<br /><br />\\"Cook on Costs 2011\\" remains an invaluable work of reference across the legal profession, including in addition to the straightforward pithy text, the tables of statutes...statutory instruments...Civil  Procedure Rules...Practice Directions about costs supplementing CPR Parts 43 to 48...non-statutory provisions...and cases, and also Michael Cook's great sense of descriptive humour.<br /><br />With the modern landscape of costs, Cook has kept pace with the dizzying change and we are all grateful to him for his wisdom and keen understanding of this perplexing aftermath of litigation- a full statement of costs suitable for the second decade of the 21st century!dizzying pace of change'- recalling that the law lords have gone, and solicitors are no longer `of the Supreme Court' but `of the Senior Courts' to site only two examples- a reference work like this needs to be published yearly. While she cares, deeply, she's not the most emotional person.  She is eminently practical and tries to apply that to what she feels, not always with the greatest results.  It's when she's not thinking about things that I think we get the clearest glimpse into her heart.  So while there were times that I wanted to smack her and tell her to stop trying to rationalize everything, I still completely stood by her while she worked through all these very difficult feelings and thoughts.<br /><br />In The Hunger Games the cast of players was relatively few. 1594488843|5.0|Sometimes in the direct sales industry, we joke about \\"More carrots! Less sticks!\\" when working to develop promotions and programs to drive our sales forces. Another common phrase is \\"What gets rewarded, gets repeated.\\" In the book Drive: The Surprising Truth About What Motivates Us by Daniel H. Pink, the author says \\"carrots and sticks\\" as drivers of productivity are ineffective remnants of a business environment of the past.<br /><br />He spends a lot of time setting up the premise that what worked in factories, with training and management, just doesn't translate to our current service economy, an economy where we rely on the creativity of the workforce to fuel innovation and boost results. It was interesting to make connections between the things he was saying about modern work environments and the studying I was doing with my college-age son on training theory and application.<br /><br />What was inspiring about Pink's premise is the idea that what truly drives people - at work and at home - is the desire to grow and develop. This search to reach our fullest potential and be a part of something bigger than ourselves and to have real pride in our \\"product\\" is so much more life affirming than the conventional wisdom that we are all looking for more money, perks, or status. People are looking for purpose and they can find it in the most mundane tasks when they're permitted to be creative and inject pride into their work.<br /><br />In fact, his studies reveal that rewarding behavior with \\"carrots\\" seems to take the natural fun out of it. Almost as if rewarding our journey for potential with something as crass as a bonus changes things so much that we lose our inner drive. And, the studies also show that fear of loss, those terrible \\"sticks,\\" is just as ineffectual. It was fascinating to read the examples. Even as someone with a lot of experience inspiring people individually and as groups, I found myself surprised by the outcomes of well-meaning incentive programs.<br /><br />This book is an interesting and deceptively short read. Though the book is full size, much of the second half is broken up into sections for specific application. Sections like a glossary, a summary of the book, and further exploration of \\"Type I\\" approach to motivation, with the \\"I\\" standing for \\"intrinsic.\\" There is also a reading list and a \\"Type I Toolkit\\" along with other tools. Those who love the first half of the book might be interested in picking and choosing which parts of the second half they'd like to dig into.<br /><br />I'd recommend this book for anyone in business who wonders how work could be structured to encourage the most productivity from a team. This would also be a great book for someone who's feeling a little ho-hum about work and wants to explore the studies about what contributes to a happy work life and the achievement of more \\"flow\\" and satisfaction.an interesting and deceptively short read. 0385493622|2.0|Writing a layman's account of a topic in pure mathematics is much more difficult than, say, physics since one is denied the easy recourse to tantalising images of time travel, worm holes and visiting alien civilisations where all the inhabitants look like Sophie Marceau.<br /><br />Dr. Singh compensates for this shortcoming by filling the pages with a rather banally written history of some of the mathematics and mathematicians involved in the three-century effort to prove the cited theorem.  Most of this is so elementary as to be offensive to all but the most immature and uneducated reader.  To be sure, some of the important maths relating to elliptic curves can be easily understood by an alert child.  But when Dr. Singh begins to describe the remarkable relation to modular forms (the Taniyama-Shimura Conjecture), his rhetoric fails and the narrative degenerates from infantile simplicity to worthless twaddle.<br /><br />On the other hand, the book does offer the slower witted amongst us a taste of the excitement of research in pure maths and for that, alone, it is valuable.<br /><br />While the gross aspects of Wiles' proof can be understood by a competent undergraduate, the details are probably accessible to only a scarce hundred people in the world.  An excellent exposition for the budding mathematician or non-specialist is provided by van der Poorten's \\"Notes on Fermat's Last Theorem,\\" which I highly recommend.

B0012GTZFC|5.0|PROS:        * Provides a researched system of classifying organizations and businesses as \\"tribes\\" that is easy to apply.      * Has useful ideas for helping people \\"tribe up\\" and improve their relations and improve organizational relations.      * Very readable and understandable.      * Doesn't pull punches on some of the conclusions.      CONS:        * Some historical interpretations are arguable.      SUMMARY: Buy this book and read it unless you have no interest in community, leadership, and business.  In that case you're probably not even reading this blog.    Leadership books.  I've been getting tired of them ever since people started deciding \\"The Art of War\\" could by applied to businesses if you ignored all the war, killing, use of fire, and soforth in the book.  Everyone talks about Leadership in business and in the world, but as I don't see any improvement out there as the amount of lame Leadership books increase, so I assume most of these texts aren't that useful.    At the same time, I'm very interested of issues in Leadership since I don't see nearly enough of it.  I see bean-counting management, rock-star style poseurs, and exploitative jerks with a narrative.  I don't see enough leadership in business, politics, media, or more - real, rallying, directing, powerful leadership.    Tribal Leadership is the kind of book I've been waiting for.  It not only explores issue of leadership, mostly (but not entirely) dealing with business, but issues of culture, organization, and community.  In many ways its a book of applied sociology that happens to focus mostly on business.    Based on research covering a decade, the book lays out a very clear thesis:       1. Humans naturally form tribes.     2. These tribes can be classified into 5 types each with a unique attitude towards life, and become more functional as you move from Type 1 to Type 5.     3. It is possible to coach people and groups to \\"tribe up\\" the scale to become more cohesive, functional, andproductive (and in some cases at least less pathological)      The book is split between describing the theories, and describing how people and groups can advance from lower to higher Tribal levels.  Each chapter leads naturally to the next, and handy checklists and bulletpoints help you keep track of important ideas.  This clear focus and organization makes the book easy to read, refer to, and use.    As for the theory itself?  It's simple and intuitive  Essentially there are five tribal types, each defined by an attitude of members:  Level 1 - \\"Life Sucks\\" - pathological, gang-like, angry.  Level 2 - \\"My Life Sucks\\" - a mix of learned helplessness, bitterness.  Level 3 - \\"I'm Great\\" - Productive and dynamic but egocentric.  Level 4 - \\"We're Great\\" - tribe-oriented, creative, productive, tight.  Level 5 - \\"Life Is Great\\" - Big-picture, tribe-connecting.    You can probably guess right now which level you and your friends and co-workers function at (hint: you're probably also wrong).    The theory itself is extremely applicable in my experience, and the authors give extensive information to help you understand where you and your various organizations fit on the tribal scale.  The clear boundaries of levels, straightforward explanations, and explanations of the classifications helps you use this theory and see the sheer lack of B.S.  Just be prepared for a few ego-bruises because most people think they function higher than they do (and this book will puncture your illusions).    The theory comes with tips, advice, and directions for raising tribal level of people and organization.  These sections are straightforward with excellent detail, from things to try, to signs to look for to identify personal progress.  Again there's a refreshing lack of B.S. here.    So is the book flawless?  No.  There's a few moments of historical reference and metaphor that seem stretched or that I disagree with.  There could be some better explanation of techniques at a few points.    These are minor concerns.    Here's what you need to know about this book- you should read it unless you have a reason not to, like a lack of money or being currently dead.    How much did I like this book?  I've given two copies as gifts and my Kindle edition is filled with notes, I've joined a group to discuss it, founded another, and am discussing applying it's philosophy with other people.  Yeah, I was impressed.    This is a must-read

1594488843|5.0|Drive isn't just about the surprising truth about what motivates us, it's also about the alarming truth about how STAGGERINGLY WRONG we have been about what we thought motivates us.<br /><br />I've long maintained that there's no such thing as attempting to motivate anyone.  That, motivation is self-directed.  That the only thing an outsider can do is present a person with a choice and that the person then motivates themselves towards one or another decision.<br /><br />However, what Dan Pink does in Drive is provide a blueprint for how to create an environment for continual self-motivation where the means and the ends are somewhat circular: autonomy, mastery and purpose.<br /><br />All three are components of what really motivates us, but all three are also the means by which to help us be, do, and have a fulfilling work and life.  Dan Pink doesn't just expose what really motivates us, he explodes many long-held myths about what we believe to be motivating, but actually turn out to be dreadfully counter-productive, not just de-motivating.<br /><br />The good news is that these obsolete myths about motivation in the workplace are barely one hundred years old.  That means that we haven't lived with them for but an atomic fraction of the human experience.  The bad news is that these last hundred years have been the absolute most astonishingly productive era in human history and these myths have seemed to work well enough that they've become ubiquitous things of academia, literature, business and government policy.<br /><br />Current titans of business, government and industry have built their empires on these myths because they seem to work for most people most of the time.  The only problem is that they also lead to many of the business, educational and societal issues of our time which are too-easily side-stepped and ignored.  Furthermore, at least two generations of workers and soon-to-be workers are rejecting these myths.  Business, government, and industry will need to re-frame their notions of what motivates us.  Those forward-thinking among them will become the new titans, only now, these new titans will be a more positive place to work and a more positive influence on humanity.<br /><br />It's painful to look at places where I've done work and to see how deeply flawed they've been in the light of Dan Pink's Drive.  So much time lost.  So much unhappiness.  So many unfulfilled possibilities.  So much wasted.<br /><br />Dan provides practice tools and guides at the end of this book to help take his investigation and put it into the workplace.  I, for one, intend on being part of the new Drive.  Business, government and industry can wait no longer to put the truth behind 21st century human motivation into the workplace and into our lives.<br /><br />My rating is not because of the scholarly and balanced investigation, but because the urgency with which people should pay attention to the book in the business world.  A more critical look at the book would find that there's more going on than meets the eye with respect to motivation.<br /><br />A few times while reading, the thought crossed my mind that some conclusions are overstated, some connections are over-emphasized, and a few relationships are over-simplified.  That, in short, there's often a much more complicated and complex set of interrelated attributes contributing to a situation, and, that how research studies play out don't always translate into the workplace -- where there are nearly unstoppable forces already firmly in place.  In a few other places I noted that Dan paints a black and white picture of right and wrong proportions where there are often many more shades of many other colors and many possible shades situationally \\"right\\".<br /><br />I believe Dan's motivation may have as much to do with jolting business out of a lull of complacency as it does with communicating new ideas.  Were his book to represent all sides on each of his points, it would be too long, unreadable, not enjoyable, an academic book, not a business book, and it would have a much weaker position to stake.<br /><br />Therefore, the short-comings of the book do not negate the conclusions nor the urgency with which our businesses, governments, and industries need to take a cold, hard look at how we're treating our people and begin to match our needs as humans with how we're handled in the workforce.would have a much weaker position to stake. It's beautifully written and - while I'm happily married - many of the parts of her journey - when she's talking about how she felt as she was getting divorced - I could relate to because I wasn't ALWAYS happily married and it took me YEARS to find MY happiness...    If you're a person who doesn't care about \\"fru-fru-ness\\" of other's and their emotions, then no this book isn't for you. But if you enjoy listening to others talk about their pleasures and figuring out in life what made THEM happy then read this book!
031603438X|5.0|If you are a Rolling Stones fan, or even a fan of rock and roll, you'll want to read this book.  While Keith Richards' Life comes up short in many respects, it is long on a whole bunch of other stuff.  The early development years of the Rolling Stones is covered quite well, and much of the song writing and development is also present.  It does lack in the song development aspect somewhat, but over all Life, from a rock and roll history aspect, is a treat.      I give Life 5 stars, not because it is extremely well written, but because of what it represents and who wrote it.  Keith Richards has written some of the most memorable songs and hooks and guitar lines in rock and roll history.  It would be impossible for him to write an autobiography and a song dissection in one book; thankfully he didn't try to do that.  What he did do was give us a bit of both. I've always been intrigued by many of the Stones songs, particularly their 60s era hits.  Songs like \\"Paint It Black\\" \\"19th Nervous Breakdown\\" \\"(I Can't Get No) Satisfaction\\" \\"Get Off Of my Cloud\\" \\"Jumpin' Jack Flash\\" and \\"Street Fighting Man\\" are all 60s classics.  They are the songs that I wanted very much to learn about.  Unfortunately, I didn't get my wish answered.  But I did get a measure of it, and for that I am grateful.      I've anticipated this book almost all of my life, and I am actually quite surprised that it has arrived.  Who would have thought the world's most popular drug addict would actually write a book?  I've been reading with some dismay many of the negative comments about the book.  My advice is to get over it and realize that Keith Richards is not a book writer; he's a guitarist and a songwriter. Appreciate the fact that as you read it, you can actually visualize and hear Keith saying what has been written.  While it's true he used a ghost writer, the tone of the book, the feel of it, is all Keith Richards.  That's a treat.    Since about the late '70s, I realized that the Stones are not somuch about Mick Jagger as they are about Keith Richards.  The book, Life, affirms my belief.  Jagger, who according to Richards, seized control of the band during Richards' drug years and has steered it ever since, with Keith Richards being sidelined as a guitarist/songwriter and resident drug addict.  This book explains why much of the Stones music recorded after 1981 sucks.  I've often wondered how it is that the Stones could write and record such classics, one right after another, and then fall into an abysmal series of bad stuff and pretty much stay there.  It's clear that it's Jagger leadership that lacked vision and musical knowledge that encased one of the greatest bands in the world into a mire of technically sound musical slop.  It also explains why Keith Richards' solo efforts are better than the Stones releases from 1991 forward.  What I got out of reading Life is that Mick Jagger has no musical vision; he's all about image and money, whereas Keith is all about music, period; well, and some ego massaging.    The book does have problems, most of which have been covered by other reviewers.  For me, the disappointment comes in two areas.  The first is that Keith ignores so much discussion on the development of their albums and hit singles.  The only album he talks about in length is Exile On Main Street.  The rest of the albums get a passing mention, if that.  I've so often wondered about the development of Beggars Banquet, Some Girls, and Tattoo You.  Little to no answers in Life.  He also pretty much ignores Bill Wyman.  It's no wonder the guy quit, he was probably never treated like an equal, and he certainly gets zero praise in this book.  I had to wonder why that is.  The second area that was disappointing is the amount of space he allotted to drug usage.  Far too many pages and pages about drug abuse.  I think I understood it the first 50 pages, I didn't need another 50.        If I learned anything at all from this book it is that Keith Richards is probably a jerk.  He probably deserved what happened to him in losing his control over the band, and the way he treats other people is reprehensible.  He brags about some of his misdeeds, like an immature middle school boy who gets caught doing something stupid and hasn't learned his mistakes.  I'd venture to say that if Keith Richards hadn't learned how to play guitar he would have added up to zero.

Dobbs. Unconscionably ignored by the Academy, yet giving the unvarnished performance of his career, Bogart dares to be unlikable even as an out of work laborer hitting up the same American for repeated hand-outs. His descent into paranoia and madness, signalled by his references to himself in the third person and his rants against the rest of humanity, is graphically positioned in the richly detailed black and white shot on location universe of both urban and rural Mexico.  While not free of racist stereotypes, most notably el bandito of Rodolpho Bedoya and his infamous \\"badges\\" comments, \\"Treasure\\" leaves an indelible impression on the viewer and would influence future films such as \\"The Wild Bunch\\" and \\"Butch Cassidy and the Sundance Kid\\", 20 years later.

B000035Z3K|4.0|Just some thoughts...<br /><br />Dracula (1931) is a classic for the ages.  Bela Lugosi's portrayal of Count Dracula has become the enduring and iconic image of the king of all vampires.  I find this black and white movie to be dark and creepy with decent special effects for 1931.  Castle Dracula is especially creepy.  The original movie has no musical score.  This movie was made before musical scores became the norm in movies.  This movie's slow pacing, lack of musical score, and slow deliverance of lines is a reflection of the time period that it was made in - that is, it was made at the end of the silent era and beginning of the sound era, so the movie was filmed in the style and pace of how a silent movie would be filmed.<br /><br />A musical score was added in 1998 by composer Philip Glass.  This DVD allows you to watch the movie without the score or with the score.  I find the Glass score, not surprisingly, very unique.  His music seems a little offbeat to begin with, and his score for this movie is no exception.  I give his score 3 1/2 stars out of 5, which I would say is fair to pretty good.  I find that some of the music doesn't fit certain scenes very well.  The music seems too powerful in scenes where it should be tamer and seems to have a drive and rhythm that's awkward much of the time.  Glass is not the right kind of composer for a movie like this.  His style is just too offbeat and awkward.<br /><br />Also included is the Spanish version of the movie, made at the same time as the American version.  I actually think the Spanish version is slightly better than the American version due to its better camera angles, slightly better atmosphere, higher energy level, and better pacing.  The Spanish version is 1 hour and 44 minutes, whereas the American version is 1 hour and 15 minutes.  This DVD also includes a documentary called \\"The Road To Dracula\\", commentary from film historian David J. Skal, the theatrical trailer, and more.<br /><br />The acting is pretty good.  Lugosi defined the classic Dracula character.  His image has held up for some 80 years.  When people dress up as Dracula for Halloween, it always seems to be the Lugosi version.<br /><br />This was the first ever Dracula movie and many more versions have followed in the last 80 years.  My personal favorites are the 1958, 1979, and 1992 movies, but this 1931 movie is the one that started it all.  Highly recommended.acula character.  His image has held up for some 80 years. B0000CNY27|4.0|Over the years, John Carpenter's Escape From New York (1981) has become a cult favorite.  It's B-movie science fiction, with crude special effects that are now part of the film's charm.  The movie has an interesting premise and a solid and very talented cast who really bring to life, this dark vision of the future.  Kurt Russell, the onetime star of Disney fare like The Computer Wore Tennis Shoes (1969), is perfect as the notorious Snake Plissken, a criminal with an eye patch, raspy voice, and a reputation for getting things done.<br /><br />Manhattan island in 1997 has become a penal colony, and the President's plane has crash landed somewhere near what was the financial district.  Police commissioner Bob Hauk (Lee Van Cleef) recruits Plissken for a rescue mission, where in return for recovering the Commander in Chief, he would earn his freedom.  The catch is that Snake has less than 23 hours to bring the President (Donald Pleasance) out alive, or charges planted in his neck will explode and kill him.  Plissken enters the city's air space in a glider, and lands on one of the towers of the World Trade Center.  He begins his search using a tracking device that homes in on a bracelet that the President is wearing.<br /><br />Escape From New York could easily have been a more violent and brutal adventure, but instead it is more atmospheric, than action packed.  Plissken navigates the streets, inhabited by the creepy lowlife that come out at night, and learns that the President has been taken prisoner by The Duke of New York (Isaac Hayes), an underworld leader.  Snake catches a huge break when he encounters Cabbie (Ernest Borgnine), a cab driver who takes him to see \\"Brain\\" Close (Harry Dean Stanton), and his girlfriend Maggie (Adrienne Barbeau).  Brain manufactures gasoline for The Duke, and also has information about an escape route from the city, via the 69th Street Bridge.  Plisskin convinces Brain to betray the Duke, and help him rescue the President.<br /><br />After being somewhat sedate, the level of action cranks up in the third act, as the rescue party attempts to save the President.  One of the more memorable scenes, is Snake's cage match to the death, where it is revealed how Plissken got his moniker.  The chase on the bridge is serviceable, but at a very small scale, and again far less thrilling than it could have been.  Despite the limitations, John Carpenter (Halloween) utilizes his resources well, delivering a film that entertains, as Snake enjoys the last laugh.<br /><br />Beloved by many, Escape From New York probably could have been a stronger film, but it is quite a fun ride for what it is.  If the film were ever remade, it is quite easy to envision it with slick high tech gadgets, CG special effects, and lots of carnage.  If it ever were to happen, one would hope that the film would capture some of the same campy style, and that the cast would even be half as good as the original, who make the most of what is basically a comic book adventure.<br /><br />Carpenter and Russell initially collaborated on the TV movie Elvis (1979), and this would be the first of three films they would make together during the 80's, the second being the classic sci-fi remake The Thing (1982), followed by Big Trouble in Little China (1986).  The two disc special edition of Escape From New York, has the most extra content, and probably provides the most bang for the buck, including two commentaries, which are not present on the Blu-ray version.  As usual, Carpenter's commentary with Russell are very informal and entertaining.sedate, the level of action cranks up in the third act, as the rescue party attempts to save the President.  One of the more memorable scenes, is Snake's cage match to the death, where it is revealed how Plissken got his moniker.  The chase on the bridge is serviceable, but at a very small scale, and again far less thrilling than it could have been.  Despite the limitations, John Carpenter (Halloween) utilizes his resources well, delivering a film that entertains, as Snake enjoys the last laugh.                 Beloved by many, Escape From New York probably could have been a stronger film, but it is quite a fun ride for what it is.
